      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">
        Sport
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Namibia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Sport">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Namibia" title="Sport in Namibia">
        Sport in Namibia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union_in_Namibia" title="Rugby union in Namibia">
        Rugby union in Namibia
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Namibia_Rugby_Team.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="900" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Namibia_Rugby_Team.jpg/150px-Namibia_Rugby_Team.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Namibia_Rugby_Team.jpg/225px-Namibia_Rugby_Team.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Namibia_Rugby_Team.jpg/300px-Namibia_Rugby_Team.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Namibia_Rugby_Team.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Namibia_national_rugby_union_team" title="Namibia national rugby union team">
          Namibia rugby team
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The most popular sport in Namibia is
       <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">
        association football
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Namibia_national_football_team" title="Namibia national football team">
        Namibia national football team
       </a>
       qualified for the
       <a href="/wiki/2008_Africa_Cup_of_Nations" title="2008 Africa Cup of Nations">
        2008 Africa Cup of Nations
       </a>
       but has yet to qualify for any
       <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">
        World Cups
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The most successful national team is the
       <a href="/wiki/Namibia_national_rugby_union_team" title="Namibia national rugby union team">
        Namibian rugby team
       </a>
       , having competed in five separate World Cups. Namibia were participants in the
       <a href="/wiki/1999_Rugby_World_Cup" title="1999 Rugby World Cup">
        1999
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/2003_Rugby_World_Cup" title="2003 Rugby World Cup">
        2003
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/2007_Rugby_World_Cup" title="2007 Rugby World Cup">
        2007
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/2011_Rugby_World_Cup" title="2011 Rugby World Cup">
        2011
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/2015_Rugby_World_Cup" title="2015 Rugby World Cup">
        2015
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Rugby_World_Cup" title="Rugby World Cup">
        Rugby World Cups
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">
        Cricket
       </a>
       is also popular, with the
       <a href="/wiki/Namibia_national_cricket_team" title="Namibia national cricket team">
        national side
       </a>
       having played in the
       <a href="/wiki/2003_Cricket_World_Cup" title="2003 Cricket World Cup">
        2003 Cricket World Cup
       </a>
       . In December 2017, Namibia Cricket reached the final of the Cricket South Africa (CSA) Provincial One Day Challenge for the first time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
        <a href="#cite_note-132">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In February 2018 Namibia will host the ICC World Cricket League Division 2 with Namibia, Kenya, UAE, Nepal, Canada and Oman to compete for the final two ICC Cricket World Cup Qualifier positions in Zimbabwe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
        <a href="#cite_note-133">
         [133]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Netball" title="Netball">
        Netball
       </a>
       is popular as well.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inline_hockey" title="Inline hockey">
        Inline hockey
       </a>
       was first played in 1995 and has also become more and more popular in the last years. The Women's inline hockey National Team participated in the 2008
       <a href="/wiki/FIRS_Inline_Hockey_World_Championships" title="FIRS Inline Hockey World Championships">
        FIRS World Championships
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Namibia is the home for one of the toughest footraces in the world, the Namibian ultra marathon.
      </p>
      <p>
       The most famous athlete from Namibia is
       <a href="/wiki/Frankie_Fredericks" title="Frankie Fredericks">
        Frankie Fredericks
       </a>
       , sprinter in the 100 and 200 m events. He won four Olympic silver medals (1992, 1996) and also has medals from several World Athletics Championships. He is also known for humanitarian activities in Namibia and beyond. Golfer
       <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Dodds" title="Trevor Dodds">
        Trevor Dodds
       </a>
       won the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Greensboro_Open" title="Greater Greensboro Open">
        Greater Greensboro Open
       </a>
       in 1998, one of 15 tournaments in his career. He achieved a career high world ranking of 78th in 1998. Professional cyclist and Namibian Road Race champion
       <a href="/wiki/Dan_Craven" title="Dan Craven">
        Dan Craven
       </a>
       represented Namibia at the
       <a href="/wiki/2016_Summer_Olympics" title="2016 Summer Olympics">
        2016 Summer Olympics
       </a>
       in both the road race and individual time trial. Boxer
       <a href="/wiki/Julius_Indongo" title="Julius Indongo">
        Julius Indongo
       </a>
       is the unified WBA, IBF, and IBO world champion in the
       <a href="/wiki/Light_welterweight" title="Light welterweight">
        Light welterweight
       </a>
       division.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Swakopmund_Skydiving_Club&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Swakopmund Skydiving Club (page does not exist)">
        Swakopmund Skydiving Club
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Swakopmund" title="Swakopmund">
        Swakopmund
       </a>
       was founded in 1974, and operates from
       <a href="/wiki/Swakopmund_Airport" title="Swakopmund Airport">
        Swakopmund Airport
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Media">
        Media
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Namibia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Media">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Namibia" title="Media of Namibia">
        Media of Namibia
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Although Namibia's population is fairly small, the country has a diverse choice of media; two TV stations, 19 radio stations (without counting community stations), 5 daily newspapers, several weeklies and special publications compete for the attention of the audience. Additionally, a mentionable amount of foreign media, especially South African, is available. Online media are mostly based on print publication contents. Namibia has a state-owned Press Agency, called
       <a href="/wiki/Namibia_Press_Agency" title="Namibia Press Agency">
        NAMPA
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothe_134-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothe-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first newspaper in Namibia was the German-language
       <i>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Windhoeker_Anzeiger&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Windhoeker Anzeiger (page does not exist)">
         Windhoeker Anzeiger
        </a>
       </i>
       , founded 1898. Radio was introduced in 1969, TV in 1981. During German rule, the newspapers mainly reflected the living reality and the view of the white German-speaking minority. The black majority was ignored or depicted as a threat. During South African rule, the white bias continued, with mentionable influence of the Pretoria government on the "South West African" media system. Independent newspapers were seen as a menace to the existing order, critical journalists threatened.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothe_134-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothe-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
        <a href="#cite_note-135">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Links_136-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Links-136">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The daily newspapers include the private publications
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Namibian" title="The Namibian">
         The Namibian
        </a>
       </i>
       (English and other languages),
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Die_Republikein" title="Die Republikein">
         Die Republikein
        </a>
       </i>
       (Afrikaans),
       <a href="/wiki/Allgemeine_Zeitung_(Namibia)" title="Allgemeine Zeitung (Namibia)">
        Allgemeine Zeitung
       </a>
       (German) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Namibian_Sun" title="Namibian Sun">
         Namibian Sun
        </a>
       </i>
       (English) as well as the state-owned
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/New_Era_(Namibia)" title="New Era (Namibia)">
         New Era
        </a>
       </i>
       (predominantly English). Except for the largest newspaper,
       <i>
        The Namibian
       </i>
       , which is owned by a trust, the other mentioned private newspapers are part of the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Democratic_Media_Holdings&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Democratic Media Holdings (page does not exist)">
        Democratic Media Holdings
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothe_134-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothe-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Other mentionable newspapers are the tabloid
       <i>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Informant%C3%A9&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Informanté (page does not exist)">
         Informanté
        </a>
       </i>
       owned by
       <i>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Trustco_Group_Holdings&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Trustco Group Holdings (page does not exist)">
         TrustCo
        </a>
       </i>
       , the weekly
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Windhoek_Observer" title="Windhoek Observer">
         Windhoek Observer
        </a>
       </i>
       , the weekly
       <a href="/wiki/Namibia_Economist" title="Namibia Economist">
        Namibia Economist
       </a>
       , as well as the regional
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Namib_Times&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Namib Times (page does not exist)">
        Namib Times
       </a>
       . Current affairs magazines include
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Insight_Namibia" title="Insight Namibia">
         Insight Namibia
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Vision2030_Focus_magazine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Vision2030 Focus magazine (page does not exist)">
         Vision2030 Focus magazine
        </a>
       </i>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Prime_FOCUS&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Prime FOCUS (page does not exist)">
         Prime FOCUS
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sister_Namibia" title="Sister Namibia">
         Sister Namibia
        </a>
       </i>
       Magazine stands out as the longest running NGO magazine in Namibia, while
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Namibia_Sport" title="Namibia Sport">
         Namibia Sport
        </a>
       </i>
       is the only national sport magazine. Furthermore, the print market is complemented with party publications, student newspapers and PR publications.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothe_134-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothe-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The broadcasting sector is dominated by the state-run
       <a href="/wiki/Namibian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Namibian Broadcasting Corporation">
        Namibian Broadcasting Corporation
       </a>
       (NBC). The
       <a href="/wiki/Public_broadcasting" title="Public broadcasting">
        public broadcaster
       </a>
       offers a TV station as well as a "National Radio" in English and nine language services in locally spoken languages. The nine private radio stations in the country are mainly English-language channels, except for Radio Omulunga (Oshiwambo) and Kosmos 94.1 (Afrikaans). Privately held
       <a href="/wiki/One_Africa_Television" title="One Africa Television">
        One Africa TV
       </a>
       has competed with NBC since the 2000s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothe_134-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothe-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oneafricabackground_137-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-oneafricabackground-137">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Compared to neighbouring countries, Namibia has a large degree of media freedom. Over the past years, the country usually ranked in the upper quarter of the
       <a href="/wiki/Press_Freedom_Index" title="Press Freedom Index">
        Press Freedom Index
       </a>
       of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reporters_without_Borders" title="Reporters without Borders">
        Reporters without Borders
       </a>
       , reaching position 21 in 2010, being on par with Canada and the best-positioned African country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
        <a href="#cite_note-138">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=African_Media_Barometer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="African Media Barometer (page does not exist)">
        African Media Barometer
       </a>
       shows similarly positive results.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       However, as in other countries, there is still mentionable influence of representatives of state and economy on media in Namibia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothe_134-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothe-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2009, Namibia dropped to position 36 on the Press Freedom Index.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
        <a href="#cite_note-139">
         [139]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2013, it was 19th.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
        <a href="#cite_note-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2014 it ranked 22nd
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
        <a href="#cite_note-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Media and journalists in Namibia are represented by the Namibian chapter of the
       <a href="/wiki/Media_Institute_of_Southern_Africa" title="Media Institute of Southern Africa">
        Media Institute of Southern Africa
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Editors%27_Forum_of_Namibia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Editors' Forum of Namibia (page does not exist)">
        Editors' Forum of Namibia
       </a>
       . An independent media ombudsman was appointed in 2009 to prevent a state-controlled media council.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothe_134-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothe-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Education">
        Education
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Namibia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Education">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:42817_06.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="434" data-file-width="640" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/42817_06.JPG/220px-42817_06.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/42817_06.JPG/330px-42817_06.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/42817_06.JPG/440px-42817_06.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:42817_06.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Secondary school students
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Namibia" title="Education in Namibia">
        Education in Namibia
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_schools_in_Namibia" title="List of schools in Namibia">
        List of schools in Namibia
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Namibia has free education for both primary and secondary education levels. Grades 1–7 are primary level, grades 8–12 are secondary. In 1998, there were 400,325 Namibian students in primary school and 115,237 students in secondary schools. The pupil-teacher ratio in 1999 was estimated at 32:1, with about 8% of the GDP being spent on education.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
        <a href="#cite_note-142">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Curriculum development, educational research, and professional development of teachers is centrally organised by the National Institute for Educational Development (NIED) in Okahandja.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
        <a href="#cite_note-143">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Most schools in Namibia are state-run, but there are some private schools, which are also part of the country's education system. There are four teacher training universities, three colleges of agriculture, a police training college, and two universities:
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Namibia" title="University of Namibia">
        University of Namibia
       </a>
       (UNAM) and
       <a href="/wiki/Namibia_University_of_Science_and_Technology" title="Namibia University of Science and Technology">
        Namibia University of Science and Technology
       </a>
       (NUST).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Health">
        Health
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Namibia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Health">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Namibia" title="Health in Namibia">
        Health in Namibia
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_Namibia" title="HIV/AIDS in Namibia">
        HIV/AIDS in Namibia
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">
        Life expectancy
       </a>
       at birth is estimated to be 64 years in 2017 – among the lowest in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
        <a href="#cite_note-144">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Namibia launched a National Health Extension Programme in 2012
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
        <a href="#cite_note-145">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
       deployment 1,800 (2015) of a total ceiling of 4,800 health extension workers trained for six months in community health activities including first aid, health promotion for disease prevention, nutritional assessment and counseling, water sanitation and hygiene practices, HIV testing and community-based antiretroviral treatment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
        <a href="#cite_note-146">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Namibia faces non-communicable disease burden. The Demographic and Health Survey (2013) summarizes findings on elevated blood pressure, hypertension, diabetes and obesity:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Among eligible respondents age 35–64, more than 4 in 10 women (44 percent) and men (45 percent) have elevated blood pressure or are currently taking medicine to lower their blood pressure.
       </li>
       <li>
        Forty-nine percent of women and 61 percent of men are not aware that they have elevated blood pressure. • Forty-three percent of women and 34 percent of men with hypertension are taking medication for their condition.
       </li>
       <li>
        Only 29 percent of women and 20 percent of men with hypertension are taking medication and have their blood pressure under control.
       </li>
       <li>
        Six percent of women and 7 percent of men are diabetic; that is, they have elevated fasting plasma glucose values or report that they are taking diabetes medication. An additional 7 percent of women and 6 percent of men are prediabetic.
       </li>
       <li>
        Sixty-seven percent of women and 74 percent of men with diabetes are taking medication to lower their blood glucose.
       </li>
       <li>
        Women and men with a higher-than-normal body mass index (25.0 or higher) are more likely to have elevated blood pressure and elevated fasting blood glucose.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_147-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-:3-147">
          [147]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The HIV epidemic remains a public health issue in Namibia despite significant achievements made by the Ministry of Health and Social Services to expand HIV treatment services.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
        <a href="#cite_note-148">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2001, there were an estimated 210,000 people living with HIV/AIDS, and the estimated death toll in 2003 was 16,000. According to the 2011 UNAIDS Report, the epidemic in Namibia "appears to be leveling off."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
        <a href="#cite_note-149">
         [149]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As the HIV/AIDS epidemic has reduced the working-aged population, the number of orphans has increased. It falls to the government to provide education, food, shelter and clothing for these orphans.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
        <a href="#cite_note-150">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A Demographic and Health Survey with an HIV biomarker was completed in
       <a class="external text" href="https://dhsprogram.com/pubs/pdf/FR298/FR298.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        2013
       </a>
       and served as the fourth comprehensive, national-level population and health survey conducted in Namibia as part of the global Demographic and Health Surveys (DHS) programme. The DHS observed important characteristics associated to the HIV epidemic:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Overall, 26 percent of men age 15–49 and 32 percent of those age 50–64 have been circumcised. HIV prevalence for men age 15–49 is lower among circumcised (8.0 percent) than among uncircumcised men (11.9 percent). The pattern of lower HIV prevalence among circumcised than uncircumcised men is observed across most background characteristics. For each age group, circumcised men have lower HIV prevalence than those who are not circumcised; the difference is especially pronounced for men age 35–39 and 45–49 (11.7 percentage points each). The difference in HIV prevalence between uncircumcised and circumcised men is larger among urban than rural men (5.2 percentage points versus 2.1 percentage points).
       </li>
       <li>
        HIV prevalence among respondents age 15–49 is 16.9 percent for women and 10.9 percent for men. HIV prevalence rates among women and men age 50–64 are similar (16.7 percent and 16.0 percent, respectively).
       </li>
       <li>
        HIV prevalence peaks in the 35–39 age group for both women and men (30.9 percent and 22.6 percent, respectively). It is lowest among respondents age 15–24 (2.5–6.4 percent for women and 2.0–3.4 percent for men).
       </li>
       <li>
        Among respondents age 15–49, HIV prevalence is highest for women and men in Zambezi (30.9 percent and 15.9 percent, respectively) and lowest for women in Omaheke (6.9 percent) and men in Ohangwena (6.6 percent).
       </li>
       <li>
        In 76.4 percent of the 1,007 cohabiting couples who were tested for HIV in the 2013 NDHS, both partners were HIV negative; in 10.1 percent of the couples, both partners were HIV positive; and 13.5 percent of the couples were discordant (that is, one partner was infected with HIV and the other was not).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_147-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-:3-147">
          [147]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       As of 2015, the Ministry of Health and Social Services and UNAIDS produced a Progress
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.unaids.org/sites/default/files/country/documents/NAM_narrative_report_2015.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        Report
       </a>
       in which
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNAIDS" title="UNAIDS">
        UNAIDS
       </a>
       projected HIV prevalence among 15 – 49 year olds at 13.3% [12.2% – 14.5%] and an estimated 210 000 [200 000 – 230 000] living with HIV.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
        <a href="#cite_note-151">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">
        malaria
       </a>
       problem seems to be compounded by the AIDS epidemic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_152-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-152">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Research has shown that in Namibia the risk of contracting malaria is 14.5% greater if a person is also infected with HIV.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_152-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-152">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The risk of death from malaria is also raised by approximately 50% with a concurrent HIV infection.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korenromp_153-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Korenromp-153">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The country had only 598 physicians in 2002.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
        <a href="#cite_note-154">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Graduate+School+of+International+Relations+and+Pacific+Studies&amp;rft.atitle=Semi-Presidential+Systems%3A+Dual+Executive+and+Mixed+Authority+Patterns&amp;rft.date=2005-09&amp;rft.aulast=Shugart&amp;rft.aufirst=Matthew+S%C3%B8berg&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdss.ucsd.edu%2F~mshugart%2Fsemi-presidentialism.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANamibia">

            </span>

         <li id="cite_note-Dual-11">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           ^
           <a href="#cite_ref-Dual_11-0">
            <sup>
             <i>
              <b>
               a
              </b>
             </i>
            </sup>
           </a>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Dual_11-1">
            <sup>
             <i>
              <b>
               b
              </b>
             </i>
            </sup>
           </a>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <cite class="citation journal">
            Shugart, Matthew Søberg (December 2005).
            <a class="external text" href="https://link.springer.com/content/pdf/10.1057%2Fpalgrave.fp.8200087.pdf" rel="nofollow">
             "Semi-Presidential Systems: Dual Executive And Mixed Authority Patterns"
            </a>
            <span style="font-size:85%;">
             (PDF)
            </span>
            .
            <i>
             French Politics
            </i>
            . Palgrave Macmillan Journals.
            <b>
             3
            </b>
            (3): pp. 323–351.
            <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
             doi
            </a>
            :
            <a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1057%2Fpalgrave.fp.8200087" rel="nofollow">
             10.1057/palgrave.fp.8200087
            </a>
            <span class="reference-accessdate">
             . Retrieved
             <span class="nowrap">
              4 September
             </span>
             2016
            </span>
            .
            <q>
             Of the contemporary cases, only four provide the assembly majority an unrestricted right to vote no confidence, and of these, only two allow the president unrestricted authority to appoint the prime minister. These two, Mozambique and Namibia, as well as the Weimar Republic, thus resemble most closely the structure of authority depicted in the right panel of Figure 3, whereby the dual accountability of the cabinet to both the president and the assembly is maximized. (...) Namibia allows the president to dissolve
             <i>
              [the assembly]
             </i>
             at any time but places a novel negative incentive on his exercise of the right: He must stand for a new election at the same time as the new assembly elections.
            </q>
           </cite>

            <span style="display:none;">

         <li id="cite_note-26">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-26">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Drechsler, Horst (1980). The actual number of deaths in the limited number of battles with the Germany Schutztruppe (expeditionary force) were limited; most of the deaths occurred after fighting had ended. The German military governor
           <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lotha_von_Trotha&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lotha von Trotha (page does not exist)">
            Lotha von Trotha
           </a>
           issued an explicit extermination order, and many Herero died of disease and abuse in detention camps after being taken from their land. A substantial minority of Herero crossed the Kalahari desert into the British colony of Bechuanaland (modern-day
           <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">
            Botswana
           </a>
           ), where a small community continues to live in western Botswana near to border with Namibia.
           <i>
            Let us die fighting
           </i>
           , originally published (1966) under the title
           <i>
            Südwestafrika unter deutsche Kolonialherrschaft
           </i>
           . Berlin: Akademie-Verlag.
          </span>
         </li>
